Another idea: Use the concept of the Simson line. The Simson line of a point on the circumcircle of a triangle is the line containing the feet of perpendiculars from that point to the sides. It is known that the Simson line of a point X is parallel to the line joining X to the orthocenter of the triangle? Not exactly, but there is a relation: The Simson line of X is the pedal line, and it is also the line that is the image of the Steiner line under a homothety with center X and factor 1/2. The Steiner line of X passes through the orthocenter H of the triangle. So if we take X = H' (the reflection of H across MN), then the Steiner line of X passes through H, and the Simson line of X is parallel to the Steiner line. So the Simson line of X is parallel to the line through H and X? Not.

Given the time, I think the most practical is to present a coordinate proof that works for all parameters by using the substitution we derived for general r. We can do the algebra in a systematic way, perhaps using computer algebra, but we have to do it manually. However, we can try to find a simpler parameterization. Note that we have s = R-r, t = R+r. And we also have that the circles intersect, so there exists A on both circles. In our coordinate system with M=(0,0), N=(1,0), we have r and R are parameters. We can also express the intersection points in terms of the angles that A makes with the x-axis. Since A is on Ω, we can set A = (r cosθ, r sinθ). Then the condition that A is on Γ gives R^2 = r^2 + d^2 - 2dr cosθ, with d=1. So R^2 = r^2 + 1 - 2r cosθ. So R = √(r^2 + 1 - 2r cosθ). That's fine.

Now, C = (-r,0), D = (1+R,0). So X = (1+R-r)/2. Then P = (X, -X (1+cosθ)/ sinθ) = (X, -X cot(θ/2)). So P = (X, -X cot(θ/2)). Now, we need to find F. We have v = P-A = (X - r cosθ, -X cot(θ/2) - r sinθ). And A-N = (r cosθ - 1, r sinθ). We can compute (A-N)·v. Let's do that in terms of θ and X.

We have: sinθ = 2 sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2), cosθ = 2 cos^2(θ/2)-1 = 1-2 sin^2(θ/2). Also, cot(θ/2)=cos(θ/2)/sin(θ/2). Then v = (X - r cosθ, -X cos(θ/2)/sin(θ/2) - 2r sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2) = -cos(θ/2)[ X/sin(θ/2) + 2r sin(θ/2) ] = -cos(θ/2)[ (X + 2r sin^2(θ/2))/ sin(θ/2) ].

Now, compute dot product (A-N)·v = (r cosθ - 1)(X - r cosθ) + r sinθ * (-X cot(θ/2) - r sinθ) = (r cosθ - 1)(X - r cosθ) - r sinθ X cot(θ/2) - r^2 sin^2θ.

Now, substitute cosθ = 1-2 sin^2(θ/2), sinθ = 2 sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2). Then r cosθ - 1 = r(1-2 sin^2(θ/2)) - 1 = r-2r sin^2(θ/2) -1. And X - r cosθ = X - r(1-2 sin^2(θ/2)) = X - r + 2r sin^2(θ/2). Also, r sinθ X cot(θ/2) = r * 2 sin(θ/2) cos(θ/2) * X * (cos(θ/2)/sin(θ/2)) = 2r X cos^2(θ/2). And r^2 sin^2θ = r^2 * 4 sin^2(θ/2) cos^2(θ/2) = 4r^2 sin^2(θ/2) cos^2(θ/2). So:

(A-N)·v = (r-2r sin^2(θ/2)-1)(X - r + 2r sin^2(θ/2)) - 2r X cos^2(θ/2) - 4r^2 sin^2(θ/2) cos^2(θ/2).

Let's denote u = sin^2(θ/2). Then cos^2(θ/2)=1-u. So:

r-2r u -1 = (r-1) - 2r u.
X - r + 2r u = (X - r) + 2r u.
So product = ((r-1)-2r u)((X-r)+2r u). Expand: = (r-1)(X-r) + (r-1)*2r u -2r u (X-r) -4r^2 u^2. Then add the other terms: -2r X (1-u) -4r^2 u (1-u) = -2r X +2r X u -4r^2 u +4r^2 u^2. So total (A-N)·v = (r-1)(X-r) + 2r(r-1)u -2r(X-r)u -4r^2 u^2 -2r X +2r X u -4r^2 u +4r^2 u^2 = (r-1)(X-r) -2r X + 2r(r-1)u -2r(X-r)u +2r X u -4r^2 u + ( -4r^2 u^2 +4r^2 u^2 ) = (r-1)(X-r) -2r X + 2r(r-1)u -2r(X-r)u +2r X u -4r^2 u.

Now, note that (r-1)(X-r) = (r-1)X - (r-1)r = (r-1)X - r(r-1). So = (r-1)X - r(r-1). Then add -2r X: total from these two = (r-1)X - r(r-1) -2r X = (r-1-2r)X - r(r-1) = (-r-1)X - r(r-1) = -(r+1)X - r(r-1). So (A-N)·v = -(r+1)X - r(r-1) + 2r(r-1)u -2r(X-r)u +2r X u -4r^2 u.

But u = sin^2(θ/2). And X = (1+R-r)/2. Also, R = √(r^2+1-2r cosθ) = √(r^2+1-2r(1-2u)) = √(r^2+1-2r+4r u) = √((r-1)^2 + 4r u). So R^2 = (r-1)^2 + 4r u. Then X = (1+R-r)/2 = (1-r+R)/2. We have:

M = (0,0), N = (1,0).

Define:

r = radius of Ω, R = radius of Γ, with r<R and R-r<1<R+r.

So y_P = - ((1+t)√(1-s^2))/(2√(t^2-1)). So y_P = - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)). That's our expression.

Now, define v = P-A = (X-x0, y_P-y0). Compute X-x0 = (1+s)/2 - (1-st)/2 = (1+s-1+st)/2 = (s+st)/2 = s(1+t)/2. So v_x = s(1+t)/2.

So v_y = - t(1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)). So v = ((1+t)/2) ( s, - t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ) = ((1+t)/2) w, where w = ( s, - t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ).

Now, we have u_F = (t-1)/r = 2(t-1)/(t-s). So F = A + u_F v = A + ((t-1)/r) v = A + ((t-1)/r)*((1+t)/2) w = A + ((t^2-1)/(2r)) w.

Now, note that w is a vector. We can also compute the coordinates of F explicitly in terms of s and t:

F_y = y0 + ((t^2-1)/(2r)) * ( - t √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) ) = y0 - (t^2-1) t √(1-s^2) / (2r √(t^2-1)) = y0 - (t^2-1) t √(1-s^2) / ((t-s) √(t^2-1)) = y0 - t √(1-s^2) √(t^2-1) / (t-s) because (t^2-1)/√(t^2-1)=√(t^2-1). So F_y = - (t+s)√(t^2-1)√(1-s^2) / (2(t-s)). That's a nice expression.

Let's search memory: I recall a problem from the IMO Shortlist 2011 G2 or something: "Let Ω and Γ be circles with centers M and N, respectively, such that the radius of Ω is less than the radius of Γ. Suppose Ω and Γ intersect at two distinct points A and B. Line MN intersects Ω at C and Γ at D, so that C, M, N, D lie on MN in that order. Let P be the circumcentre of triangle ACD. Line AP meets Ω again at E and meets Γ again at F. Let H be the orthocentre of triangle PMN. Prove that the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circumcircle of triangle BEF." I think the solution uses complex numbers with the circle Ω as the unit circle, and then defines some variables. I found a solution online: "Place the configuration in the complex plane with M=0, and let the circle Ω be the unit circle. Then A=e^{iθ}, B=e^{-iθ}. Let N=d (real). Then C=-1, D=d+R, where R=|e^{iθ}-d|. Then P = (d+R-1)/2 - i (d+R-1)/2 * cot(θ/2). Then the orthocenter H of triangle PMN is P - 2i y0, where y0 = ((P_x-d/2)^2+P_y^2-(d/2)^2)/(2P_y). Then the line through H parallel to AP is tangent to the circle through B, E, F. This can be shown by computing the power of H with respect to the circle (BEF) and showing that it equals the square of the distance from H to the line." That's essentially coordinate proof.

M_BP = ( (B_x+P_x)/2, (B_y+P_y)/2 ) = ( ((1-st)/2 + (1+s)/2)/2 = ( (1-st+1+s)/4 ) = ( (2 + s - st)/4 ) = (2 + s(1-t))/4 = (2 - s(t-1))/4. And y: (B_y+P_y)/2 = ( -K/2 + P_y )/2 = ( -K/2 + P_y )/2.

But we also need the slope of BP: (P_y - B_y)/(P_x - B_x). Compute P_x - B_x = (1+s)/2 - (1-st)/2 = (1+s-1+st)/2 = (s+st)/2 = s(1+t)/2. So P_x - B_x = s(1+t)/2. And P_y - B_y = - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)) - ( -K/2 ) = - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)) + K/2. Since K = √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2), we have √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) = K/(t^2-1). Thus P_y - B_y = (K/2) * (t-2)/(t-1). So slope BP = (P_y-B_y)/(P_x-B_x) = [K/2 * (t-2)/(t-1)] / [s(1+t)/2] = K (t-2) / [s (1+t)(t-1)].

The perpendicular bisector of BP has slope = -1/(slope BP) = - s (1+t)(t-1) / [K (t-2)].

Given the difficulty, I'll try to complete the algebraic proof for the general case using the substitution we have. Let's compute O by solving the linear equations using the method of elimination, but we can use the fact that the points B, P, F are all expressed in terms of s and t, and we might find that O has a simple expression. Perhaps we can compute O by using the formula for circumcenter of three points in complex numbers, but we can do it with coordinates.

Let's define:

B = (B_x, B_y) = ( (1-st)/2, - (1/2)K ), where K = √(t^2-1)√(1-s^2).
P = (P_x, P_y) = ( (1+s)/2, - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)) ).
F = (F_x, F_y) = ( (1-st)/2 + s(t^2-1)/(t-s), - (t+s)K/(2(t-s)) ).

Note that K appears in B_y, P_y, F_y. Also, note that √(1-s^2) and √(t^2-1) can be expressed in terms of K: √(1-s^2) = K/√(t^2-1), √(t^2-1) = K/√(1-s^2). But we can keep them.

Now, let's compute the vectors:

BP = (P_x - B_x, P_y - B_y) = ( s(1+t)/2, P_y - B_y ). We already computed P_y - B_y = K (t-2)/(2(t-1)). So BP = ( s(1+t)/2, K (t-2)/(2(t-1)) ).

BF = (F_x - B_x, F_y - B_y) = ( s(t^2-1)/(t-s), F_y - B_y ). Compute F_y - B_y = - (t+s)K/(2(t-s)) - ( -K/2 ) = K[ - (t+s)/(2(t-s)) + 1/2 ] = K/2 [ - (t+s)/(t-s) + 1 ] = K/2 [ (-t-s + t-s)/(t-s) ] = K/2 [ (-2s)/(t-s) ] = - K s/(t-s). So BF = ( s(t^2-1)/(t-s), - K s/(t-s) ) = s/(t-s) ( t^2-1, -K ).

Now, we have the three points. The circumcenter O is the intersection of perpendicular bisectors. The perpendicular bisector of BP: set of points X such that (X - M_BP) dot BP = 0. Similarly for BF.

Let’s compute M_BP = ( (B_x+P_x)/2, (B_y+P_y)/2 ) = ( ( (1-st)/2 + (1+s)/2 )/2 = ( (2 + s - st)/4 ) = (2 + s(1-t))/4 = (2 - s(t-1))/4. And y: (B_y+P_y)/2 = ( -K/2 + P_y )/2 = ( -K/2 + P_y )/2.

We already have P_y = - (1+t)√(1-s^2)/(2√(t^2-1)). Write that in terms of K: √(1-s^2)/√(t^2-1) = K/(t^2-1). So P_y = - (1+t)K/(2(t^2-1)). Then B_y+P_y = -K/2 - (1+t)K/(2(t^2-1)) = -K/2 [ 1 + (1+t)/(t^2-1) ] = -K/2 [ (t^2-1 + 1+t)/(t^2-1) ] = -K/2 [ (t^2+t)/(t^2-1) ] = -K/2 [ t(t+1)/((t-1)(t+1)) ] = -K t/(2(t-1)). So (B_y+P_y)/2 = -K t/(4(t-1)). So M_BP = ( (2 - s(t-1))/4, -K t/(4(t-1)) ).

Now, the perpendicular bisector of BP: set of points X such that (X - M_BP) dot BP = 0. So (Ox - M_BP_x)*BP_x + (Oy - M_BP_y)*BP_y = 0.

BP_x = s(1+t)/2. BP_y = K (t-2)/(2(t-1)).

So equation: (Ox - (2 - s(t-1))/4) * (s(1+t)/2) + (Oy - ( -K t/(4(t-1)) )) * (K (t-2)/(2(t-1))) = 0. Multiply both sides by 2: (Ox - (2 - s(t-1))/4) * s(1+t) + (Oy + K t/(4(t-1))) * K (t-2)/(t-1) = 0. (Equation 1)

Now, the perpendicular bisector of BF: M_BF = ( (B_x+F_x)/2, (B_y+F_y)/2 ). Compute B_x+F_x = (1-st)/2 + (1-st)/2 + s(t^2-1)/(t-s) = (1-st) + s(t^2-1)/(t-s). So (B_x+F_x)/2 = (1-st)/2 + s(t^2-1)/(2(t-s)). And B_y+F_y = -K/2 + ( - (t+s)K/(2(t-s)) ) = -K/2 [ 1 + (t+s)/(t-s) ] = -K/2 [ (t-s + t+s)/(t-s) ] = -K/2 [ (2t)/(t-s) ] = -K t/(t-s). So (B_y+F_y)/2 = -K t/(2(t-s)). Thus M_BF = ( (1-st)/2 + s(t^2-1)/(2(t-s)), -K t/(2(t-s)) ).

Now, BF = ( s(t^2-1)/(t-s), -K s/(t-s) ) = s/(t-s) ( t^2-1, -K ). So BF_x = s(t^2-1)/(t-s), BF_y = -K s/(t-s).

The perpendicular bisector of BF: (X - M_BF) dot BF = 0. So (Ox - M_BF_x)*BF_x + (Oy - M_BF_y)*BF_y = 0.

Plug in:

Ox - M_BF_x = Ox - [ (1-st)/2 + s(t^2-1)/(2(t-s)) ].

Let me try to derive the complex proof.

Set M=0, Ω: |z|=1. Let A=e^{iθ}, B=e^{-iθ}. Let N=d (real positive). Then R = |A-d| = sqrt(1+d^2-2d cosθ). Let D = d+R (since C=-1). Then X = (d+R-1)/2. Then P = X - iX cot(θ/2). In complex form, P = X(1 - i cot(θ/2)).

Actually, since the unit circle is symmetric, the chord through A with direction v has the other endpoint at the point obtained by reflecting A across the line through the origin perpendicular to v. Let's derive: If we have a line through A with direction v, then the second intersection E satisfies that the midpoint of AE lies on the line through the origin perpendicular to v. The origin is the center of the circle. So the condition is that (A+E)/2 is a real multiple of i\bar{v} (since the line through origin perpendicular to v is the set of points λ i \bar{v} for real λ). So (A+E)/2 = λ i \bar{v} for some real λ. Also, E = A + u v for some real u (since the line is param). Then (A+E)/2 = A + (u/2) v = λ i \bar{v}. So A = λ i \bar{v} - (u/2) v. Taking complex conjugate: \bar{A} = -λ i v - (u/2) \bar{v}. Since A is on unit circle, \bar{A}=1/A. Not simple.

Simplify the first term: (t^2-1)*(1-st)/2 + (t^2-1)* s(t^2-1)/(2(t-s)). And K^2 = (t^2-1)(1-s^2). So the term K^2 t/(2(t-s)) = t (t^2-1)(1-s^2)/(2(t-s)). So RHS2 = (t^2-1)(1-st)/2 + s(t^2-1)^2/(2(t-s)) + t(t^2-1)(1-s^2)/(2(t-s)).

Combine the two fractions with denominator 2(t-s): = (t^2-1)(1-st)/2 + (t^2-1)/(2(t-s)) [ s(t^2-1) + t(1-s^2) ].

Thus RHS2 = (t^2-1)(1-st)/2 + (t^2-1)/(2(t-s)) * (t-s)(st+1) = (t^2-1)(1-st)/2 + (t^2-1)(st+1)/2 = (t^2-1)/2 [ (1-st) + (st+1) ] = (t^2-1)/2 * 2 = t^2-1.
